         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
           [6]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
          Albedo
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <div class="plainlist">
          <ul>
           <li>
            0.35 (geometrical)
           </li>
           <li>
            0.17 (Bond)
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001_14-0">
             <a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001-14">
              [7]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <table style="border-spacing: 0px; width:100%; border:none; background-color:#f9f9f9; margin:0; text-align:left; line-height:1.2em; white-space:nowrap">
          <tr>
           <th style="width:33%; padding-right:0.25em; text-align:left">

            max
           </th>
          </tr>
          <tr>
           <th style="padding-left:1.0em">
            solstice
            <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-2">
             <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
              [4]
             </a>
            </sup>
           </th>
           <td>
            60 K
           </td>
           <td>
            <span class="nowrap">
             70 ± 7
            </span>
            <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
             K
            </a>
           </td>
           <td>
            89 K
           </td>
          </tr>
         </table>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">
           Apparent magnitude
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         13.9
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_Teece_1995_15-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Newton_Teece_1995-15">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         Atmosphere
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          Surface
          <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">
           pressure
          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         &lt;1–2
         <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">
          mPa
         </a>
         (10–20
         <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">
          nbar
         </a>
         )
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_chemistry#Atmospheric_composition" title="Atmospheric chemistry">
          Composition by volume
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <div class="plainlist">
          <ul>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
             carbon dioxide
            </a>
            (?)
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
             nitrogen
            </a>
            (?)
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
             methane
            </a>
            (?)
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Titania
       </b>
       is the largest of the
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">
        moons of Uranus
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites_by_diameter" title="List of natural satellites by diameter">
        eighth largest moon
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       at a diameter of 1,578 kilometres (981 mi). Discovered by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">
        William Herschel
       </a>
       in 1787, Titania is named after the
       <a href="/wiki/Titania" title="Titania">
        queen of the fairies
       </a>
       in Shakespeare's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">
         A Midsummer Night's Dream
        </a>
       </i>
       . Its orbit lies inside
       <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">
        Uranus
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania consists of approximately equal amounts of ice and
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rock
       </a>
       , and is probably differentiated into a rocky
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">
        core
       </a>
       and an icy
       <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
        mantle
       </a>
       . A layer of liquid water may be present at the
       <a href="/wiki/Core%E2%80%93mantle_boundary" title="Core–mantle boundary">
        core–mantle boundary
       </a>
       . The surface of Titania, which is relatively dark and slightly red in color, appears to have been shaped by both impacts and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenic" title="Endogenic">
        endogenic
       </a>
       processes. It is covered with numerous
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact craters
       </a>
       reaching up to 326 kilometres (203 mi) in diameter, but is less heavily cratered than
       <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">
        Oberon
       </a>
       , outermost of the five large moons of Uranus. Titania probably underwent an early endogenic resurfacing event which obliterated its older, heavily cratered surface. Titania's surface is cut by a system of enormous
       <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">
        canyons
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fault_scarp" title="Fault scarp">
        scarps
       </a>
       , the result of the expansion of its interior during the later stages of its evolution. Like all major moons of Uranus, Titania probably formed from an
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">
        accretion disk
       </a>
       which surrounded the planet just after its formation.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        Infrared
       </a>
       spectroscopy conducted from 2001 to 2005 revealed the presence of water
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        ice
       </a>
       as well as frozen
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       on the surface of Titania, which in turn suggested that the moon may have a tenuous carbon dioxide
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       with a surface pressure of about 10 nanopascals (10
       <sup>
        −13
       </sup>
       bar). Measurements during Titania's occultation of a
       <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">
        star
       </a>
       put an upper limit on the surface pressure of any possible atmosphere at 1–2 mPa (10–20 nbar).
      </p>
      <p>
       The Uranian system has been studied up close only once, by the spacecraft
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       in January 1986. It took several images of Titania, which allowed mapping of about 40% of its surface.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Titania was discovered by William Herschel on January 11, 1787, the same day he discovered Uranus's second largest moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">
        Oberon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1787_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1787-2">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1788_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1788-16">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He later reported the discoveries of four more satellites,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1798_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1798-17">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although they were subsequently revealed as spurious.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struve1848_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Struve1848-18">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For nearly fifty years following their discovery, Titania and Oberon would not be observed by any instrument other than William Herschel's,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herschel_1834_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herschel_1834-19">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although the moon can be seen from
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       with a present-day high-end amateur telescope.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_Teece_1995_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Newton_Teece_1995-15">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="729" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png/250px-Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png/375px-Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png/500px-Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titania_Earth_Moon_Comparison.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Size comparison of
         <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
          Earth
         </a>
         , the
         <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
          Moon
         </a>
         , and Titania.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       All of Uranus's moons are named after characters created by
       <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">
        William Shakespeare
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Pope" title="Alexander Pope">
        Alexander Pope
       </a>
       . The name Titania was taken from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titania_(Fairy_Queen)" title="Titania (Fairy Queen)">
        the Queen of the Fairies
       </a>
       in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">
         A Midsummer Night's Dream
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuiper_1949_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuiper_1949-20">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The names of all four satellites of Uranus then known were suggested by Herschel's son
       <a href="/wiki/John_Herschel" title="John Herschel">
        John
       </a>
       in 1852, at the request of
       <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">
        William Lassell
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell_1852_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell_1852-21">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       who had discovered the other two moons,
       <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">
        Ariel
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">
        Umbriel
       </a>
       , the year before.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell_1851_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell_1851-22">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania was initially referred to as "the first satellite of Uranus", and in 1848 was given the designation
       <b>
        <span class="nowrap">
         Uranus I
        </span>
       </b>
       by William Lassell,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell_1848_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell_1848-23">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although he sometimes used William Herschel's numbering (where Titania and Oberon are II and IV).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell_1850_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell_1850-24">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1851 Lassell eventually numbered all four known satellites in order of their distance from the planet by
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">
        Roman numerals
       </a>
       , and since then Titania has been designated
       <b>
        <span class="nowrap">
         Uranus III
        </span>
       </b>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lassell,_letter_1851_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lassell,_letter_1851-25">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Shakespeare's character's name is pronounced
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">
            eɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/j/: 'y' in 'yes'">
            j
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       , but the moon is often pronounced
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/aɪ/: 'i' in 'tide'">
            aɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">
            eɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">
            i
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       , by analogy with the familiar chemical element
       <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
        titanium
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webster_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Webster-3">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The adjectival form,
       <i>
        Titanian,
       </i>
       is homonymous with that of Saturn's moon
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       . The name
       <i>
        Titania
       </i>
       is ancient Greek in origin, meaning "Daughter of the Titans."
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">
        Orbit
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Orbit">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Titania orbits Uranus at the distance of about 436,000 kilometres (271,000 mi), being the second farthest from the planet among its five major moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [g]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Titania's orbit has a small
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        eccentricity
       </a>
       and is
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_inclination" title="Orbital inclination">
        inclined
       </a>
       very little relative to the
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        equator
       </a>
       of Uranus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orbit_5-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-orbit-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital period
       </a>
       is around 8.7 days, coincident with its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rotational_period" title="Rotational period">
        rotational period
       </a>
       . In other words, Titania is a
       <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_orbit" title="Synchronous orbit">
        synchronous
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidally_locked" title="Tidally locked">
        tidally locked
       </a>
       satellite, with one face always pointing toward the planet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania's orbit lies completely inside the Uranian
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is important, because the trailing hemispheres of satellites orbiting inside a magnetosphere are struck by magnetospheric plasma, which co-rotates with the planet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ness_Acuña_et_al._1986-28">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This bombardment may lead to the darkening of the trailing hemispheres, which is actually observed for all Uranian moons except Oberon (see below).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because Uranus orbits the Sun almost on its side, and its moons orbit in the planet's equatorial plane, they (including Titania) are subject to an extreme seasonal cycle. Both northern and southern
       <a href="/wiki/Poles_of_astronomical_bodies#Geographic_poles" title="Poles of astronomical bodies">
        poles
       </a>
       spend 42 years in a complete darkness, and another 42 years in continuous sunlight, with the sun rising close to the
       <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">
        zenith
       </a>
       over one of the poles at each
       <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">
        solstice
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       flyby coincided with the southern hemisphere's 1986 summer solstice, when nearly the entire northern hemisphere was unilluminated. Once every 42 years, when Uranus has an
       <a href="/wiki/Equinox" title="Equinox">
        equinox
       </a>
       and its equatorial plane intersects the Earth, mutual
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        occultations
       </a>
       of Uranus's moons become possible. In 2007–2008 a number of such events were observed including two occultations of Titania by Umbriel on August 15 and December 8, 2007.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_&amp;_Chanover_2009_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Miller_&amp;_Chanover_2009-29">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arlot_Dumas_et_al._2008_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arlot_Dumas_et_al._2008-30">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition_and_internal_structure">
        Composition and internal structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Composition and internal structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA00039_Titania.jpg">
         <img alt="A round spherical body with its left half illuminated. The surface has a mottled appearance with bright patches among relatively dark terrain. The terminator is slightly to the right from the center and runs from the top to bottom. A large crater with a central pit can be seen at the terminator in the upper half of the image. Another bright crater can be seen at the bottom intersected by a canyon. The second large canyon runs from the darkness at the lower-right side to visible center of the body." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1200" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/PIA00039_Titania.jpg/220px-PIA00039_Titania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/PIA00039_Titania.jpg/330px-PIA00039_Titania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/PIA00039_Titania.jpg/440px-PIA00039_Titania.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA00039_Titania.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
           Voyager 2
          </a>
          '
         </i>
         s highest-resolution image of Titania shows moderately cratered plains, enormous
         <a href="/wiki/Rift" title="Rift">
          rifts
         </a>
         and long
         <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
          scarps
         </a>
         . Near the bottom, a region of smoother plains including the crater
         <a href="/wiki/Ursula_(crater)" title="Ursula (crater)">
          Ursula
         </a>
         is split by the graben Belmont Chasma.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Titania is the largest and most massive Uranian moon, and the eighth most massive moon in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [h]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its density of 1.71 g/cm³,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jacobson_Campbell_et_al._1992-10">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which is much higher than the typical density of Saturn's satellites, indicates that it consists of roughly equal proportions of water ice and dense non-ice components;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the latter could be made of
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rock
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
        carbonaceous
       </a>
       material including heavy
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The presence of water ice is supported by
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        infrared
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscopic" title="Spectroscopic">
        spectroscopic
       </a>
       observations made in 2001–2005, which have revealed
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystalline" title="Crystalline">
        crystalline
       </a>
       water ice on the surface of the moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Water ice
       <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">
        absorption bands
       </a>
       are slightly stronger on Titania's leading hemisphere than on the trailing hemisphere. This is the opposite of what is observed on Oberon, where the trailing hemisphere exhibits stronger water ice signatures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cause of this asymmetry is not known, but it may be related to the bombardment by charged particles from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Uranus" title="Magnetosphere of Uranus">
        magnetosphere of Uranus
       </a>
       , which is stronger on the trailing hemisphere (due to the plasma's co-rotation).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The energetic particles tend to
       <a href="/wiki/Sputtering" title="Sputtering">
        sputter
       </a>
       water ice, decompose
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       trapped in ice as
       <a href="/wiki/Clathrate_hydrate" title="Clathrate hydrate">
        clathrate hydrate
       </a>
       and darken other organics, leaving a dark, carbon-rich
       <a href="/wiki/Residue_(chemistry)" title="Residue (chemistry)">
        residue
       </a>
       behind.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Except for water, the only other compound identified on the surface of Titania by infrared spectroscopy is
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       , which is concentrated mainly on the trailing hemisphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The origin of the carbon dioxide is not completely clear. It might be produced locally from
       <a href="/wiki/Carbonate" title="Carbonate">
        carbonates
       </a>
       or organic materials under the influence of the solar
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        ultraviolet
       </a>
       radiation or energetic charged particles coming from the magnetosphere of Uranus. The latter process would explain the asymmetry in its distribution, because the trailing hemisphere is subject to a more intense magnetospheric influence than the leading hemisphere. Another possible source is the
       <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">
        outgassing
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primordial_elements" title="Primordial elements">
        primordial
       </a>
       CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       trapped by water ice in Titania's interior. The escape of CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       from the interior may be related to the past geological activity on this moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania may be differentiated into a rocky
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">
        core
       </a>
       surrounded by an icy
       <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
        mantle
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If this is the case, the radius of the core 520 kilometres (320 mi) is about 66% of the radius of the moon, and its mass is around 58% of the moon’s mass—the proportions are dictated by moon's composition. The pressure in the center of Titania is about 0.58
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GPa" title="GPa">
        GPa
       </a>
       (5.8
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kbar" title="Kbar">
        kbar
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The current state of the icy mantle is unclear. If the ice contains enough ammonia or other
       <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">
        antifreeze
       </a>
       , Titania may have a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        subsurface ocean
       </a>
       at the core–mantle boundary. The thickness of this ocean, if it exists, is up to 50 kilometres (31 mi) and its temperature is around 190
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However the present internal structure of Titania depends heavily on its thermal history, which is poorly known.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">
        Surface features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Surface features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titania_(moon)_labeled.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1104" data-file-width="1013" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg/220px-Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg/330px-Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg/440px-Titania_%28moon%29_labeled.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titania_(moon)_labeled.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Titania with surface features labeled. The south pole is situated close to the unidentified bright crater below and left of the crater Jessica.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Among Uranus's moons, Titania is intermediate in brightness between the dark Oberon and Umbriel and the bright Ariel and
       <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">
        Miranda
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001-14">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface shows a strong
       <a href="/wiki/Opposition_surge" title="Opposition surge">
        opposition surge
       </a>
       : its reflectivity decreases from 35% at a phase angle of 0° (
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        geometrical albedo
       </a>
       ) to 25% at an angle of about 1°. Titania has a relatively low
       <a href="/wiki/Bond_albedo" title="Bond albedo">
        Bond albedo
       </a>
       of about 17%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Karkoschka,_Hubble_2001-14">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface is generally slightly red in color, but less red than that of
       <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">
        Oberon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1991_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bell1991-34">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, fresh impact deposits are bluer, while the smooth plains situated on the leading hemisphere near
       <a href="/wiki/Ursula_(crater)" title="Ursula (crater)">
        Ursula crater
       </a>
       and along some grabens are somewhat redder.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1991_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bell1991-34">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There may be an asymmetry between the leading and trailing hemispheres;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buratti1991_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buratti1991-36">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the former appears to be redder than the latter by 8%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [i]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, this difference is related to the smooth plains and may be accidental.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1991_34-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bell1991-34">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The reddening of the surfaces probably results from
       <a href="/wiki/Space_weathering" title="Space weathering">
        space weathering
       </a>
       caused by bombardment by charged particles and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometeorites" title="Micrometeorites">
        micrometeorites
       </a>
       over the age of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bell1991_34-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bell1991-34">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the color asymmetry of Titania is more likely related to accretion of a reddish material coming from outer parts of the Uranian system, possibly, from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_satellite" title="Irregular satellite">
        irregular satellites
       </a>
       , which would be deposited predominately on the leading hemisphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buratti1991_36-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Buratti1991-36">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Scientists have recognized three classes of geological feature on Titania:
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        craters
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chasm" title="Chasm">
        chasmata
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Canyon" title="Canyon">
        canyons
       </a>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Rupes" title="Rupes">
        rupes
       </a>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature-38">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The surface of Titania is less heavily cratered than the surfaces of either Oberon or Umbriel, which means that it is much younger.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The crater diameters reach 326 kilometers for the largest known crater,
       <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_(crater)" title="Gertrude (crater)">
        Gertrude
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS:_Titania:_Gertrude_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGS:_Titania:_Gertrude-39">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (there can be also a degraded basin of approximately the same size).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some craters (for instance,
       <a href="/wiki/Ursula_(crater)" title="Ursula (crater)">
        Ursula
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jessica_(crater)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jessica (crater) (page does not exist)">
        Jessica
       </a>
       ) are surrounded by bright impact ejecta (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rayed_crater" title="Rayed crater">
        rays
       </a>
       ) consisting of relatively fresh ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       All large craters on Titania have flat floors and central peaks. The only exception is Ursula, which has a pit in the center.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To the west of Gertrude there is an area with irregular topography, the so-called "unnamed basin", which may be another highly degraded impact basin with the diameter of about 330 kilometres (210 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania's surface is intersected by a system of enormous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Normal_fault" title="Normal fault">
        faults
       </a>
       , or scarps. In some places, two parallel scarps mark depressions in the satellite's crust,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       forming
       <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">
        grabens
       </a>
       , which are sometimes called canyons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most prominent among Titania's canyons is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messina_Chasma" title="Messina Chasma">
        Messina Chasma
       </a>
       , which runs for about 1,500 kilometres (930 mi) from the equator almost to the south pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature_38-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature-38">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The grabens on Titania are 20–50 kilometres (12–31 mi) wide and have a relief of about 2–5 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The scarps that are not related to canyons are called rupes, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Rousillon_Rupes" title="Rousillon Rupes">
        Rousillon Rupes
       </a>
       near Ursula crater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature_38-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-USGS:_Titania_Nomenclature-38">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The regions along some scarps and near Ursula appear smooth at
       <i>
        Voyager
       </i>
       's image resolution. These smooth plains were probably resurfaced later in Titania's geological history, after the majority of craters formed. The resurfacing may have been either endogenic in nature, involving the eruption of fluid material from the interior (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryovolcanism" title="Cryovolcanism">
        cryovolcanism
       </a>
       ), or, alternatively it may be due to blanking by the impact ejecta from nearby large craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The grabens are probably the youngest geological features on Titania—they cut all craters and even smooth plains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The geology of Titania was influenced by two competing forces:
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact crater
       </a>
       formation and endogenic resurfacing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The former acted over the moon's entire history and influenced all surfaces. The latter processes were also global in nature, but active mainly for a period following the moon's formation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They obliterated the original heavily cratered terrain, explaining the relatively low number of impact craters on the moon's present-day surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additional episodes of resurfacing may have occurred later and led to the formation of smooth plains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alternatively smooth plains may be ejecta blankets of the nearby impact craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most recent endogenous processes were mainly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">
        tectonic
       </a>
       in nature and caused the formation of the canyons, which are actually giant cracks in the ice crust.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cracking of the crust was caused by the global expansion of Titania by about 0.7%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croft_1989_40-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Croft_1989-40">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

A large canyon runs from the darkness at the lower-right side to visible center of the body." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="150" height="440" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/91/Messina_Chasma.jpg" width="150"/>
        </a>

       <tr>
        <th colspan="5" scope="row">
         Surface features on Titania are named for characters from Shakespeare's works.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobell_&amp;_Masursky_1987_41-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Strobell_&amp;_Masursky_1987-41">
           [31]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">
        Atmosphere
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The presence of carbon dioxide on the surface suggests that Titania may have a tenuous seasonal atmosphere of CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       , much like that of the Jovian moon
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Callisto" title="Atmosphere of Callisto">
        Callisto
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [j]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other gases, like
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">
        methane
       </a>
       , are unlikely to be present, because Titania's weak gravity could not prevent them from escaping into space. At the maximum temperature attainable during Titania's
       <a href="/wiki/Summer_solstice" title="Summer solstice">
        summer solstice
       </a>
       (89 K), the
       <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">
        vapor pressure
       </a>
       of carbon dioxide is about 300 μPa (3 nbar).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On September 8, 2001, Titania
       <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">
        occulted
       </a>
       a bright star (
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=HIP106829&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="HIP106829 (page does not exist)">
        HIP106829
       </a>
       ) with a
       <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">
        visible magnitude
       </a>
       of 7.2; this was an opportunity to both refine Titania's diameter and
       <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">
        ephemeris
       </a>
       , and to detect any extant atmosphere. The data revealed no atmosphere to a surface pressure of 1–2 mPa (10–20 nbar); if it exists, it would have to be far thinner than that of
       <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">
        Triton
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This upper limit is still several times higher than the maximum possible surface pressure of the carbon dioxide, meaning that the measurements place essentially no constraints on parameters of the atmosphere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The peculiar geometry of the Uranian system causes the moons' poles to receive more
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">
        solar energy
       </a>
       than their equatorial regions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because the vapor pressure of CO
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       is a steep function of temperature,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       this may lead to the accumulation of carbon dioxide in the low-latitude regions of Titania, where it can stably exist on high albedo patches and shaded regions of the surface in the form of ice. During the summer, when the polar temperatures reach as high as 85–90 K,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009_7-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Widemann_Sicardy_et_al._2009-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       carbon dioxide
       <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(phase_transition)" title="Sublimation (phase transition)">
        sublimates
       </a>
       and migrates to the opposite pole and to the equatorial regions, giving rise to a type of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">
        carbon cycle
       </a>
       . The accumulated carbon dioxide ice can be removed from cold traps by magnetospheric particles, which sputter it from the surface. Titania is thought to have lost a significant amount of carbon dioxide since its formation 4.6 billion years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006_27-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grundy_Young_et_al._2006-27">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">
        Origin and evolution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Titania is thought to have formed from an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accretion_disc" title="Accretion disc">
        accretion disc
       </a>
       or subnebula; a disc of gas and dust that either existed around Uranus for some time after its formation or was created by the giant impact that most likely gave Uranus its large
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obliquity" title="Obliquity">
        obliquity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mousis_2004_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mousis_2004-43">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The precise composition of the subnebula is not known; however, the relatively high density of Titania and other Uranian moons compared to the
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        moons of Saturn
       </a>
       indicates that it may have been relatively water-poor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [k]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Significant amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">
        nitrogen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
        carbon
       </a>
       may have been present in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">
        carbon monoxide
       </a>
       and N
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       instead of
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       and methane.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mousis_2004_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mousis_2004-43">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The moons that formed in such a subnebula would contain less water ice (with CO and N
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       trapped as a clathrate) and more rock, explaining their higher density.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Titania's accretion probably lasted for several thousand years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mousis_2004_43-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mousis_2004-43">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The impacts that accompanied accretion caused heating of the moon's outer layer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988-45">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The maximum temperature of around 250 K (−23 °C) was reached at a depth of about 60 kilometres (37 mi).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988-45">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the end of formation, the subsurface layer cooled, while the interior of Titania heated due to decay of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">
        radioactive elements
       </a>
       present in its rocks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cooling near-surface layer contracted, while the interior expanded. This caused strong
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extensional_stress" title="Extensional stress">
        extensional stresses
       </a>
       in the moon's crust leading to cracking. Some of the present-day canyons may be a result of this. The process lasted for about 200 million years,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hillier_&amp;_Squyres_1991_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hillier_&amp;_Squyres_1991-46">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       implying that any endogenous activity ceased billions of years ago.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The initial
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
        accretional heating
       </a>
       together with continued decay of radioactive elements were probably strong enough to melt the ice if some antifreeze like ammonia (in the form of
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">
        ammonia hydrate
       </a>
       ) or
       <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">
        salt
       </a>
       was present.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988_45-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Squyres_Reynolds_et_al._1988-45">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Further melting may have led to the separation of ice from rocks and formation of a rocky core surrounded by an icy mantle. A layer of liquid water (ocean) rich in dissolved ammonia may have formed at the core–mantle boundary.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutectic_temperature" title="Eutectic temperature">
        eutectic temperature
       </a>
       of this mixture is 176 K (−97 °C).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006_33-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hussmann_Sohl_et_al._2006-33">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If the temperature dropped below this value, the ocean would have subsequently frozen. The freezing of the water would have caused the interior to expand, which may have been responsible for the formation of the majority of the canyons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the present knowledge of Titania's geological evolution is quite limited.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Uranus" title="Exploration of Uranus">
        Exploration of Uranus
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       So far the only close-up images of Titania have been from the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       probe, which photographed the moon during its flyby of Uranus in January 1986. Since the closest distance between
       <i>
        Voyager 2
       </i>
       and Titania was only 365,200 km (226,900 mi),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stone_1987_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Stone_1987-47">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the best images of this moon have a spatial resolution of about 3.4 km (only Miranda and Ariel were imaged with a better resolution).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plescia_1987_35-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Plescia_1987-35">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The images cover about 40% of the surface, but only 24% was photographed with the precision required for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_mapping" title="Geological mapping">
        geological mapping
       </a>
       . At the time of the flyby, the southern hemisphere of Titania (like those of the other moons) was pointed towards the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       , so the northern (dark) hemisphere could not be studied.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986_13-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Smith_Soderblom_et_al._1986-13">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       No other spacecraft has ever visited the Uranian system or Titania, and no mission is currently planned. One possibility, now discarded, was to send
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       on from Saturn to Uranus in an extended mission. Another mission concept proposed was the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uranus_orbiter_and_probe" title="Uranus orbiter and probe">
        Uranus orbiter and probe
       </a>
       concept, evaluated around 2010. Uranus was also examined as part of one trajectory for a precursor interstellar probe concept,
       <a href="/wiki/Innovative_Interstellar_Explorer" title="Innovative Interstellar Explorer">
        Innovative Interstellar Explorer
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A Uranus orbiter
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       was listed as the third priority for a NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">
        Flagship mission
       </a>
       by the NASA
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_Science_Decadal_Survey" title="Planetary Science Decadal Survey">
        Planetary Science Decadal Survey
       </a>
       , and conceptual designs for such a mission are currently being analyzed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Titania_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-1">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-1">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Along the terminator are visible the moon's largest known
           <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
            impact crater
           </a>
           ,
           <a href="/wiki/Gertrude_(crater)" title="Gertrude (crater)">
            Gertrude
           </a>
           , at upper right and several enormous canyon-like
           <a href="/wiki/Graben" title="Graben">
            grabens
           </a>
           (the
           <a href="/wiki/Messina_Chasmata" title="Messina Chasmata">
            Messina Chasmata
           </a>
           above,
           <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Belmont_Chasma&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Belmont Chasma (page does not exist)">
            Belmont Chasma
           </a>
           near bottom) at lower right.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
